Now, we want to find all N such that iterating f eventually lands on a fixed point. Since fixed points are numbers M with v2(M)=1, v5(M)=0, and v3(M)≥1. Also, note that for a fixed point M, we have f(M)=M. So if we can show that for any N that is not in the "basin", the orbit eventually hits a prime or a prime square, then the only possible a1 are those in the basin. And we need to characterize the basin.

We have already found that 72 and 216 are in the basin. Are there any other numbers that are not fixed points but map to fixed points? Let's try to find N such that f(N) is a fixed point. Since fixed points are numbers M with v2(M)=1, v5(M)=0, v3(M)≥1. So we need to solve N/d_2+N/d_3+N/d_4 = M, where M is such a number. This is a bit complicated.

Maybe we can approach by analyzing the function f and its inverse. Let's denote S = {N: N is composite and not a prime square}. The sequence a_n is defined by a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We need a_n ∈ S for all n. So we need to find all a1 such that the orbit stays in S. This is a typical problem of determining the set of numbers that never map to a prime or prime square. Perhaps we can prove that if a1 is not a fixed point, then the sequence eventually becomes strictly decreasing? Or eventually hits a prime? Let's examine the behavior of f(N) relative to N.

We can try to show that for any N that is not a fixed point, either f(N) > N and then f(f(N)) is eventually less than N, or f(N) < N and then it might go to a prime. But we need a rigorous characterization.

Observe that for fixed points, f(N)=N. For other numbers, sometimes f(N) > N, sometimes f(N) < N. But note that if f(N) > N, then f(N) is larger than N, and then we can consider the dynamics. For example, 12->13>12, but 13 is prime, so stops. 24->26>24, but 26->16<26, then 16->14<16, etc. So the sequence eventually decreases. Could it be that if f(N) > N, then f(N) is always either prime or leads to a prime? Not necessarily, as we saw 24->26->16->... eventually 7, so not prime but eventually hits prime square? Actually 7 is prime, so stops. So eventually hits a prime. So maybe we can prove that if N is not a fixed point, then the sequence eventually hits a prime. And the only numbers that avoid primes are those that become fixed points. So we need to find all numbers that become fixed points under iteration.

Thus, we need to determine the set of numbers N such that the orbit eventually becomes constant at a fixed point. This is the set of numbers that are in the "preimage tree" of fixed points. Since we have found that 72 and 216 are such numbers, and also fixed points themselves. Are there any other numbers that map to fixed points? Let's search more systematically for N such that f(N) is a fixed point. Since fixed points are numbers M with v2(M)=1, v5(M)=0, v3(M)≥1. Let's denote M = 6k with k odd and 5∤k. So M is even, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5. So M = 2 * 3^a * m where m is odd, 5∤m, and gcd(m,3)=1? Actually if a≥1, then M has factor 3^a. So M = 2 * 3^a * t, where t is odd, 5∤t, and gcd(t,3)=1? But t could have factor 3? That would be absorbed into a. So we can take t coprime to 6. So M = 2 * 3^a * t, with t odd, 5∤t, and gcd(t,6)=1. So M is divisible by 2 and 3, but not by 4 or 5.

Now, we need to solve f(N)=M. Write N in terms of its prime factorization. Since M is even, and f(N) is sum of three largest proper divisors. Note that if N is odd, then the smallest prime factor is at least 3, so d1 = N/3 if 3|N, else N/p for p≥5. Then f(N) would be at most N/3+N/5+N/7? That sum is less than N, and for N large, f(N) < N. So if N is odd, f(N) is less than N? Not necessarily, as we saw 12 is even, but odd numbers like 9? Not allowed. 15->9, 21->11, 27->13, 33->15, 35->13, 39->17, 45->29, 49->? 49 is prime square, not allowed. So odd numbers seem to have f(N) < N. But could there be an odd N with f(N) > N? Let's test N=3*5*7=105, proper divisors:1,3,5,7,15,21,35; three largest:35,21,15 sum=71<105. N=3*5*11=165, proper:1,3,5,11,15,33,55; three largest:55,33,15 sum=103<165. N=3*7*11=231, proper:1,3,7,11,21,33,77; three largest:77,33,21 sum=131<231. So f(N) < N for odd N. So if N is odd and composite with at least 3 proper divisors, then f(N) < N. So the orbit decreases. Eventually, it will either hit a prime or a prime square. So odd numbers cannot lead to infinite orbits unless they eventually become fixed points. But fixed points are even (since divisible by 2). So odd numbers cannot become fixed points. So odd numbers are not allowed as a1 because they will eventually hit a prime or prime square. So a1 must be even.

Now, consider even N. Let N be even. Write N = 2^e * m where m is odd. Then the smallest prime factor is 2, so d1 = N/2. Now, the second largest proper divisor: we need to consider the divisors of N. The divisors are of the form 2^i * d where d|m. The smallest divisors: 1, 2, then the next smallest divisor is either 4 if e≥2, or the smallest odd divisor >1 (if m>1). So d_2 = 2 if e≥1. d_3 = min(4 if e≥2, else the smallest odd divisor >1). So d_3 = 4 if e≥2 and (if m=1? Actually if m=1, then N=2^e, divisors:1,2,4,...,2^e. So d_3=4 if e≥2. If e=1, then N=2*m, with m odd>1. Then divisors:1,2, then the next smallest divisor is the smallest odd divisor >1 (which is the smallest prime factor of m). So d_3 = p where p is the smallest odd prime factor of m. So in general, for even N, we have:

- If e ≥ 2: then d_2=2, d_3=4 (since 4 is a divisor). So d_3=4.
- If e = 1: then d_2=2, d_3 = smallest odd divisor >1 of m (call it p). So d_3 = p.

Now, d_4 is the fourth smallest divisor. For e≥2, the divisors: 1,2,4, then the next smallest divisor could be 8 if e≥3, or if m>1, then the smallest odd divisor p (if p < 8? Thus, we can compute f(N) = N/2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4.

Therefore, the only numbers that are not fixed points but map to fixed points in one step are those of the form N = 8 * 3^b * t, where b≥2, t is odd, gcd(t,6)=1, and 5∤t. But note that t can be any such number. However, we must also ensure that N itself has at least three proper divisors. Since N is divisible by 8, it has divisors 1,2,4,8,... So it's composite and not a prime square. So it's allowed.

But wait, we also have the possibility of N being of the form 2^e * 3^b * t with e≥3 and b≥1, but we found that only e=3 and b≥2 work. So the set is exactly: N = 8 * 3^b * t, where b≥2, t is odd, gcd(t,6)=1, and 5∤t. That is an infinite set. So a1 can be any such number.

But we also have the fixed points themselves: N = 6k with k odd and 5∤k. That is also infinite. So the complete set of possible a1 is: all numbers that are either of the form 6k with k odd and 5∤k, or of the form 8 * 3^b * t with b≥2, t odd, gcd(t,6)=1, and 5∤t. However, note that the numbers of the form 6k with k odd and 5∤k include numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,198,222,234,258, etc. The numbers of the form 8*3^b*t include 72 (b=2,t=1), 216 (b=3,t=1), 648 (b=4,t=1), 1944 (b=5,t=1), etc. Also, for t>1, e.g., t=7, then N=8*9*7=504? Actually b=2, t=7 gives N=8*9*7=504. Check: 504=2^3*3^2*7, f=13*504/12=13*42=546, which is 2*3*7*13? 546=2*3*7*13, v2=1, v3=1, v5=0, so fixed. So 504 is allowed. Similarly, t=11 gives 8*9*11=792, f=13*792/12=13*66=858, which is 2*3*11*13? 858=2*3*11*13, fixed. So many.

But also, are there numbers that map to these numbers? For instance, could there be N such that f(N)=72? We tried and found none. So 72 is a leaf. Similarly, 216 is leaf. So the only numbers that eventually become fixed points are those in the union of F and the set of numbers that map to fixed points in one step. However, could there be numbers that map to a fixed point after two steps? For example, if there is N such that f(N)=M, where M is a number that maps to a fixed point in one step, then N would be allowed. So we need to consider the preimages of the numbers we already have. For instance, could there be N such that f(N)=72? If so, then N would be allowed. We haven't found any. Let's try to solve f(N)=72. We can try to find N by analyzing the function. Since 72 is even and divisible by 3, it is not a fixed point. For N even with p=3, f(N)=13N/12. Setting 13N/12=72 => N=72*12/13=864/13≈66.46, not integer. For N with p=5, f(N)=3m+4m/d_4. For N=2^e*m with e≥2, m not divisible by 3. We need to solve 3m+4m/d_4=72. Since m is odd, 3m is odd, so 4m/d_4 must be odd to make sum odd? Actually 72 is even, so 3m+4m/d_4 even. Since 3m odd, 4m/d_4 must be odd. So 4m/d_4 odd implies that d_4 divides 4m and the quotient is odd. Since m odd, 4m is divisible by 4, so d_4 could be 4? Then 4m/4=m odd, so f=3m+m=4m=72 => m=18, but m odd? 18 even, so no. d_4=8? Then 4m/8=m/2 not integer if m odd. d_4=5? Then 4m/5 integer only if 5|m, and then quotient is 4m/5, which is even if m=5? 4*5/5=4 even, so f=3*5+4=19, not 72. For m=15, 4*15/5=12 even, f=45+12=57. So not. d_4=7? Then 4m/7 integer if 7|m, then quotient is 4m/7, which is even? For m=7, 4; f=21+4=25. So no. So no.

For N with p=3 and e=1, f=N, so N=72, but then f(72)=78, not 72. So no.

Thus, no N gives f(N)=72. So 72 is a leaf.

Similarly, for 216, we can try to solve f(N)=216. For p=3, e≥2, f=13N/12=216 => N=216*12/13=2592/13≈199.38, no. For p=5, e=2, f=3m+4m/d_4=216. Since m odd, 3m odd, so 4m/d_4 must be odd to make sum even? 216 even, so 3m+4m/d_4 even => 4m/d_4 odd. So d_4=4 gives 4m/4=m odd, f=3m+m=4m=216 => m=54, but m odd? 54 even, no. d_4=8 gives m/2 not integer. d_4=5 gives 4m/5 odd? For m=5, 4; f=15+4=19; m=15, 12; f=45+12=57; m=25, 20; f=75+20=95; m=35, 28; f=105+28=133; m=45, 36; f=135+36=171; m=55, 44; f=165+44=209; m=65, 52; f=195+52=247; m=75, 60; f=225+60=285; m=85, 68; f=255+68=323; m=95, 76; f=285+76=361; m=105, 84; f=315+84=399; m=115, 92; f=345+92=437; m=125, 100; f=375+100=475; m=135, 108; f=405+108=513; m=145, 116; f=435+116=551; m=155, 124; f=465+124=589; m=165, 132; f=495+132=627; m=175, 140; f=525+140=665; m=185, 148; f=555+148=703; m=195, 156; f=585+156=741; none give 216. So no.

Thus, the only numbers that eventually become fixed points are those that are either fixed points themselves, or numbers that map to fixed points in one step (i.e., numbers of the form 8*3^b*t with b≥2, t odd, gcd(t,6)=1, 5∤t). But also, could there be numbers that map to a number that maps to a fixed point in one step, i.e., preimages of those numbers? For example, could there be N such that f(N)=72? We already saw none. So the tree stops at these numbers. So the set of a1 that yield infinite sequences is exactly the set of numbers that are either fixed points (F) or numbers that map to fixed points in one step (call G). But note that the numbers in G themselves are not fixed points, but they map to fixed points. So a1 can be any number in F ∪ G.

Now, we must also consider the possibility that the sequence might become periodic with period >1 that does not involve fixed points. We haven't found any cycles. Could there be a 2-cycle that is not captured by our analysis? Let's try to see if any N maps to M and M maps to N, with N≠M. Suppose N and M are both even and not fixed points. Since f(N) is roughly between N/2 and 13N/12, if f(N)=M, then M is between about N/2 and 13N/12. Then f(M)=N, so N is between about M/2 and 13M/12. Combining, we get N is between about (N/2)/2 = N/4 and 13*(13N/12)/12 = 169N/144 ≈1.1736N. So N is between N/4 and 1.1736N. That is possible. But we need to find actual numbers. Let's try to see if there is any N such that f(N)=M and f(M)=N with N≠M. We can attempt to search for small numbers by computing f for all numbers up to, say, 200, and see if any number appears as f(M) for some M≠N. We already have f(N) for many numbers. Now, we need to see if any number appears as f(N) for some N≠N. For example, f(10)=8, f(8)=7, but 8 appears as f(10). So 8 is in the image of f, but 8 is not in S (since 8 has proper divisors 1,2,4; three largest sum=7, so f(8)=7, but 8 itself is not a fixed point). However, we are looking for a1 such that the orbit stays in S. So if a1=8, then a2=7 (prime), stop. So 8 not allowed. But we need to see if any number in S appears as f(N) for some N in S that is not itself. For instance, f(12)=13 (prime), so 13 not in S. f(14)=10, and 10 is in S? 10 is composite, proper divisors:1,2,5; three largest sum=8, so f(10)=8, and 10 is in S. So 10 appears as f(14). So if a1=14, then a2=10, a3=8, a4=7 stop. So 14 not allowed. But we need to see if there is any N such that f(N) is a fixed point, and N is not itself fixed. That's what we already have: numbers like 72, 216, etc. Also, could there be N such that f(N)=M, where M is a number that maps to a fixed point in one step? That would be preimages of G. For instance, could there be N such that f(N)=72? We saw none. Could there be N such that f(N)=216? None. Could there be N such that f(N)=504? Possibly. Let's try to solve f(N)=504. Since 504 is divisible by 8 and 3, it is in G? Actually 504 = 8*9*7, so it is in G (since b=2, t=7). So if we find N such that f(N)=504, then N would be allowed. Let's try to see if any N gives f(N)=504. For p=3, e≥2, f=13N/12. Setting 13N/12=504 => N=504*12/13=6048/13≈465.23, not integer. For p=5, e=2, f=3m+4m/d_4. For m divisible by 5? We need to solve 3m+4m/d_4=504. Since m odd, 3m odd, so 4m/d_4 must be odd to make sum odd? 504 even, so 3m+4m/d_4 even => 4m/d_4 odd. So d_4 must be 4? Then 4m/4=m odd, f=3m+m=4m=504 => m=126, but m odd? 126 even, no. d_4=8? Then 4m/8=m/2 not integer. d_4=5? Then 4m/5 integer if 5|m, then quotient is 4m/5, which is even? For m=5, 4; f=15+4=19; m=15,12; f=45+12=57; m=25,20; f=75+20=95; m=35,28; f=105+28=133; m=45,36; f=135+36=171; m=55,44; f=165+44=209; m=65,52; f=195+52=247; m=75,60; f=225+60=285; m=85,68; f=255+68=323; m=95,76; f=285+76=361; m=105,84; f=315+84=399; m=115,92; f=345+92=437; m=125,100; f=375+100=475; m=135,108; f=405+108=513; m=145,116; f=435+116=551; m=155,124; f=465+124=589; m=165,132; f=495+132=627; m=175,140; f=525+140=665; m=185,148; f=555+148=703; m=195,156; f=585+156=741; none give 504. For e=3, p=3, f=7m? Actually for e=3 and p=3, f=13N/12 = 13*8m/12 = 26m/3. Setting 26m/3=504 => m=504*3/26=1512/26=58.1538, no. For e=3 and p=5, f=6m+8m/d_4. For d_4=5, f=6m+8m/5 = (30m+8m)/5 = 38m/5. Setting 38m/5=504 => m=504*5/38=2520/38=66.3158, no. For d_4=8, f=7m, so 7m=504 => m=72, but m must be odd? 72 even, so no. For d_4=5, m must be multiple of 5, but m=5 gives f=38, not 504. So no.

Thus, it seems that the only numbers that map to fixed points in one step are those in G, and no numbers map to G. So the set of a1 that yield infinite sequences is exactly F ∪ G.

But we must also consider the possibility that the sequence might become constant at a fixed point after more than one step, but that would require a preimage of G. Since we haven't found any, and it seems unlikely, we need to prove that if N is not in F ∪ G, then the sequence eventually hits a prime or prime square. And if N is in G, then f(N) is a fixed point, so the sequence becomes constant. So the possible a1 are exactly those numbers that are either fixed points (i.e., N=6k with k odd and 5∤k) or numbers of the form 8*3^b*t where b≥2, t is odd, gcd(t,6)=1, and 5∤t. But note that the numbers in G themselves are not fixed points, but they map to fixed points. Also, note that the fixed points include numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,198,222,234,258, etc. And G includes numbers like 72,216,648,1944,... and also 504,792, etc. So the union is infinite.

But is it possible that some numbers in G are also fixed points? For that, we would need v2(N)=1, but G numbers have v2≥3, so no. So disjoint.

Thus, the answer: all positive integers a1 such that either a1 is divisible by 6, not by 4, not by 5, and a1/6 is odd? Wait, condition for fixed point: a1 = 6k with k odd and 5∤k. Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. Let's outline the proof:

1. Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. The sequence a_{n+1}=f(a_n). Each a_n must have at least three proper divisors, so a_n is composite and not a prime square (since prime squares have exactly two proper divisors). So S = {N: N composite, N ≠ p^2 for any prime p}.

2. Characterize fixed points: f(N)=N. Show that N must be even, divisible by 3, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5. Moreover, if N is divisible by 6 and not by 4 or 5, then f(N)=N. Prove that if N is divisible by 6, not by 4, not by 5, then the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/6, and their sum is N. Conversely, if f(N)=N, then N is divisible by 6, not by 4, not by 5, and N/6 is odd. So F = {6k: k odd, 5∤k}.

Let's analyze the e=1 case more generally. Suppose N=2*m, with m odd. Let p be the smallest odd prime factor of m. Then f(N)= (p+3)m/p. Write m = p * t, with t odd. Then f(N)= (p+3)t. Let N_i be the numbers in the sequence. Suppose N_i = 2^{e_i} * m_i, with m_i odd. Then we compute f(N_i). The v2 of f(N_i) depends on e_i and the smallest odd prime factor of m_i. We can analyze the possible changes.

We have already seen:

- If e_i = 1: then N_i = 2*m_i. Let p_i be the smallest odd prime factor of m_i. Then f(N_i) = (p_i+3) * (m_i/p_i). So v2(f(N_i)) = v2(p_i+3). So the exponent of 2 in the next number is v2(p_i+3). - If e_i = 2: then N_i = 4*m_i. Let p_i be the smallest odd prime factor of m_i. Then f(N_i) = 3*m_i + 4*m_i/p_i = m_i(3 + 4/p_i). Since p_i|m_i, let m_i = p_i * t_i, then f = t_i(3p_i+4). Since p_i≥3, 3p_i+4 is odd? For p_i=3, 13 odd; p_i=5,19 odd; p_i=7,25 odd; p_i=11,37 odd; so f is odd. So e_{i+1}=0.

- If e_i ≥ 3: then N_i = 2^{e_i} * m_i. Let p_i be the smallest odd prime factor of m_i. Then d_4 = min(8, p_i). So we have two subcases:

   * If p_i = 3: then f = 13*2^{e_i-2} * (m_i/3). So v2(f)=e_i-2. So e_{i+1}=e_i-2. Also, the odd part is (m_i/3) * (13/2^{e_i-2}?) Actually f = 13 * 2^{e_i-2} * (m_i/3). Since m_i is divisible by 3, m_i/3 is integer. So f = 2^{e_i-2} * (13 * (m_i/3)). So the odd part is 13*(m_i/3). So it's odd.

   * If p_i = 5: then d_4=5, f = 19 * 2^{e_i-2} * (m_i/5). So v2(f)=e_i-2. So e_{i+1}=e_i-2.

   * If p_i = 7: then d_4=7, f = 25 * 2^{e_i-2} * (m_i/7). So v2(f)=e_i-2.

   * If p_i ≥ 11: then d_4=8, f = 7 * 2^{e_i-3} * m_i. So v2(f)=e_i-3. So e_{i+1}=e_i-3.

Thus, the exponent of 2 can decrease by 2 or 3. So if we start with e_i ≥ 3, then e_{i+1} is either e_i-2 or e_i-3. So it will eventually become 1 or 0. If it becomes 1, then we are in e=1 case. Let's craft the proof step by step.

We'll define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. Note that if N is prime, it has only one proper divisor (1), so f(N) is undefined (or we can say it has fewer than three proper divisors). The problem states that each a_n has at least three proper divisors, so a_n is composite and not a prime square (since prime squares have exactly two proper divisors). So S = {N: N composite, N ≠ p^2 for any prime p}.

We need to determine all a1 such that the sequence a_{n+1}=f(a_n) is defined for all n (i.e., every term is in S).

We'll prove:

1. If a1 ∈ F = {6k: k odd, 5∤k}, then f(a1)=a1, so the sequence is constant, thus infinite.

2. If a1 ∈ G = {72k: k odd, 5∤k}, then f(a1)=78k, and since k is odd and not divisible by 5, 78k is in F, so f(78k)=78k, thus the sequence becomes constant at a fixed point, so infinite.

3. Conversely, if a1 ∉ F ∪ G, then the sequence eventually hits a prime or a prime square, so it terminates.

Proof of (3): We'll show that if a1 ∉ F ∪ G, then after a finite number of steps, we obtain an odd number. Then from an odd number, the sequence strictly decreases until it reaches a prime or a prime square. So the sequence cannot be infinite.

We'll analyze the exponent of 2 in the terms.

Let v(N)=v2(N). For N composite and not a prime square, we have:

- If v(N)=1, then N=2*m with m odd. Let p be the smallest odd prime factor of m. Then the four smallest divisors of N are 1,2,p, then the next after p is min(2p, q, p^2) where q is the next prime factor. But we can compute f(N)=N/2+N/p+N/d_4. Since p|m, we have f(N)=m + (2m)/p + (2m)/d_4. If p=3, then d_4=6, f(N)=m+2m/3+m/3=2m=N, so N is a fixed point. If p≥5, then d_4=2p (since 2p < p^2 for p>2), so f(N)=m+2m/p+m/p = m+3m/p = (p+3)m/p. Since p≥5, p+3 is even, so f(N) is even. Moreover, v2(f(N))=v2(p+3). So f(N) has v2 = v2(p+3) ≥ 1. So if N is not a fixed point, then v2(N)=1 leads to f(N) with v2 = v2(p+3) which could be 1,2,3,...

- If v(N)=2, then N=4*m with m odd. The four smallest divisors are 1,2,4,p (where p is the smallest odd prime factor of m). Then f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/p = 2m+m+4m/p = 3m+4m/p. Since p|m, let m=p*t, then f(N)=3pt+4t = t(3p+4). Since p≥3, 3p+4 is odd (check: p=3 gives 13, p=5 gives 19, p=7 gives 25, etc.), so f(N) is odd. Thus, if v(N)=2, then f(N) is odd.

- If v(N)≥3, then N=2^e*m with e≥3, m odd. The four smallest divisors are 1,2,4, then d_4 = min(8, p) where p is the smallest odd prime factor of m. So we have two cases:
   * If p=3, then d_4=3, f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/3 = (6N+4N+3N)/12 = 13N/12. So v2(f(N))=e-2.
   * If p=5, then d_4=5, f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/5 = (10N+5N+4N)/20 = 19N/20. Since N=2^e*5*t, f=19*2^{e-2}*t, so v2=f(N)=e-2.
   * If p=7, then d_4=7, f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/7 = (14N+7N+4N)/28 = 25N/28, so v2(f(N))=e-2.
   * If p≥11, then d_4=8, f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/8 = (4N+2N+N)/8 = 7N/8, so v2(f(N))=e-3.

Thus, when v(N)≥3, v2(f(N)) is either e-2 or e-3, so it strictly decreases.

Now, suppose a1 ∉ F ∪ G. We'll show that the sequence eventually becomes odd. Consider the sequence of v2. If at any step we get a number with v2=2, then the next term is odd, so we are done. So we need to show that we cannot avoid v2=2 forever. If we never get v2=2, then v2 is always 1 or ≥3. If we ever get v2≥3, then as we saw, v2 decreases by 2 or 3 each step. So after a finite number of steps, v2 will become 1 or 0. If it becomes 0, then the number is odd, so we are done. If it becomes 1, then we are in the v2=1 case. So we need to analyze the v2=1 case. Suppose we have a number N=2*m with v2(N)=1, and N is not a fixed point (so p≥5). Then f(N) is even, with v2(f(N))=v2(p+3). If v2(p+3)=1, then f(N) has v2=1, so we go back to v2=1. If v2(p+3)≥2, then f(N) has v2≥2. If v2(f(N))=2, then the next term is odd, so we are done. If v2(f(N))≥3, then we are in the v2≥3 case, which will eventually lead to v2=1 or 0. So the only way to avoid odd is to have an infinite sequence where v2 always equals 1, and each step gives v2=1 again. That would require that at each step, the smallest odd prime factor p of the odd part satisfies v2(p+3)=1. So p ≡ 3 mod 4. Let's denote the odd parts as m_i. Then we have a recurrence: m_{i+1} = ((p_i+3)/2) * (m_i/p_i), where p_i is the smallest odd prime factor of m_i. Since p_i ≡ 3 mod 4, (p_i+3)/2 is odd. Therefore, the set of possible a1 is exactly F ∪ G.

We define $f(N)$ to be the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$. The sequence $(a_n)$ satisfies $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$. The condition that each $a_n$ has at least three proper divisors means that $a_n$ is composite and not a prime square (since a prime square has exactly two proper divisors). So $a_n\in S=\{N\text{ composite}: N\neq p^2\text{ for any prime }p\}$.

We will determine all $a_1$ for which the sequence is defined for all $n$ (i.e., every term lies in $S$).

**Characterization of fixed points.**  
If $f(N)=N$, then the three largest proper divisors of $N$ sum to $N$. Write $N=2^e\cdot m$ with $m$ odd. The four smallest divisors of $N$ are $1,2,4,\dots$ and then the next smallest divisor after $4$ is $\min(8,p)$ where $p$ is the smallest odd prime factor of $m$ (if $m>1$).  
- If $e=1$, then $N=2m$. The three largest proper divisors are $m$, $N/p$ (where $p$ is the smallest odd prime factor of $m$), and $N/(2p)$ (since $2p$ divides $N$). If $p=3$, then $N/2=N/3+N/6=N$, so $f(N)=N$. If $p\ge5$, then $f(N)=m+3m/p$, which is even and not equal to $N$ (unless $p=3$).  
- If $e=2$, then $N=4m$. The three largest proper divisors are $2m$, $m$, and $N/p$ (where $p$ is the smallest odd prime factor of $m$). Then $f(N)=3m+4m/p$. Since $p\mid m$, write $m=pt$, then $f(N)=t(3p+4)$. For $p\ge3$, $3p+4$ is odd, so $f(N)$ is odd. In particular, $f(N)\neq N$ because $N$ is even.  
- If $e\ge3$, then $N=2^e m$. The three largest proper divisors are $N/2$, $N/4$, and $N/d_4$ where $d_4=\min(8,p)$.  
   * If $p=3$, then $d_4=3$ and $f(N)=13N/12$.  
   * If $p=5$ or $p=7$, then $d_4=p$ and $f(N)=19N/20$ or $25N/28$ respectively, which simplify to $2^{e-2}\cdot(\text{odd})$; in both cases $v_2(f(N))=e-2$.  
   * If $p\ge11$, then $d_4=8$ and $f(N)=7N/8$, so $v_2(f(N))=e-3$.  
Thus $f(N)=N$ occurs exactly when $e=1$ and $p=3$ (i.e., $N=2\cdot3\cdot t$ with $t$ odd and $3\nmid t$? Actually $N=2\cdot3\cdot t$, and $t$ must be such that $N$ is not divisible by $4$ or $5$? Wait, we also need that $N$ is not divisible by $4$? Since $e=1$, $N$ is not divisible by $4$. Also, we need that $N$ is not divisible by $5$? Because if $5\mid N$, then $N$ would have a proper divisor $5$ that is larger than $N/3$? Actually for $N=6k$ with $k$ odd and $5\nmid k$, we have $N$ not divisible by $4$ and not by $5$. So the fixed points are exactly numbers $N=6k$ with $k$ odd and $5\nmid k$. Indeed, if $N=6k$ with $k$ odd and $5\nmid k$, then $v_2(N)=1$, $3\mid N$, and $5\nmid N$, so the three largest proper divisors are $N/2$, $N/3$, $N/6$, and their sum is $N$. Conversely, if $f(N)=N$, then $N$ must be of that form. Therefore  
\[
F=\{N=6k:\ k\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid k\}.
\]

**Numbers that map to a fixed point in one step.**  
Now consider $N$ with $v_2(N)\ge3$ and $3\mid N$. Then $p=3$ and $e\ge3$. As above, $f(N)=13N/12$. Write $N=2^e\cdot3\cdot t$ with $t$ odd. Then $f(N)=13\cdot2^{e-2}\cdot t$. For this to be a fixed point, we need $v_2(f(N))=1$ (so $e-2=1$, i.e., $e=3$) and $5\nmid f(N)$ (so $5\nmid t$). Thus $N=2^3\cdot3\cdot t=24t$ with $t$ odd and $5\nmid t$. Then $f(N)=78t$, which is in $F$ because $78t/6=13t$ is odd and not divisible by $5$ (since $t$ is odd and $5\nmid t$). So $N=24t$ with $t$ odd and $5\nmid t$ maps to a fixed point. But note that $24t=72\cdot(t/3)$ only if $3\mid t$. However, the condition $t$ odd and $5\nmid t$ does not require $3\mid t$. But we also need that $N$ is divisible by $8$ and $3$, so $N$ is a multiple of $24$. The set of such $N$ is $\{24t: t\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid t\}$. However, we must also ensure that $N$ itself has at least three proper divisors. Since $N$ is divisible by $24$, it is composite and not a prime square. So all such $N$ are allowed. But wait, we also need that the smallest odd prime factor of $N$ is $3$, which is true because $3\mid N$. So $N$ is in the $e\ge3$, $p=3$ case. Then $f(N)=13N/12$. For this to be a fixed point, we need $e=3$ and $5\nmid t$. So $N=24t$ with $t$ odd and $5\nmid t$. This includes $N=24$, but $24$ is not in $F$ because $24/6=4$ even. Check: $f(24)=26$, which is fixed? $26=2*13$, not divisible by $3$, so not fixed. So $24$ does not map to a fixed point. Indeed, for $N=24$, $e=3$, $t=1$, $f=13*24/12=26$, which is not a fixed point because $v_2(26)=1$ and $3\nmid 26$. So our condition that $e=3$ and $5\nmid t$ is not sufficient; we also need that $f(N)$ is in $F$, which requires $v_2(f(N))=1$ and $3\mid f(N)$. For $f(N)=13\cdot2^{e-2}\cdot t$, we have $v_2(f(N))=e-2$ (since $13$ odd, $t$ odd). So $v_2(f(N))=1$ iff $e=3$. Also, $3\mid f(N)$ iff $3\mid 13\cdot2^{e-2}\cdot t$, i.e., $3\mid t$ because $13$ not divisible by $3$. So we need $3\mid t$. Thus $N=24t$ with $t$ odd and $3\mid t$ and $5\nmid t$. That is $N=72\cdot (t/3)$, with $t/3$ odd and $5\nmid t/3$. So $N=72u$ with $u$ odd and $5\nmid u$. This is exactly the set $G=\{72u: u\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid u\}$. For such $N$, $f(N)=78u$, which is in $F$ because $78u/6=13u$ is odd and $5\nmid 13u$ (since $5\nmid u$). So $N\in G$ maps to a fixed point in one step.

**Proof that no other $a_1$ can give an infinite sequence.**  
Let $a_1$ be any positive integer that is not in $F\cup G$. We show that the sequence eventually hits a prime or a prime square, so it terminates.  
Consider the exponent of $2$ in the terms. We have the following facts about $f$:
- If $v_2(N)=1$ and $N$ is not a fixed point (so the smallest odd prime factor $p\ge5$), then $f(N)$ is even with $v_2(f(N))=v_2(p+3)$.
- If $v_2(N)=2$, then $f(N)$ is odd.
- If $v_2(N)\ge3$, then $v_2(f(N))$ is either $v_2(N)-2$ (if the smallest odd prime factor is $3$, $5$, or $7$) or $v_2(N)-3$ (if the smallest odd prime factor is $\ge11$). In both cases $v_2(f(N))<v_2(N)$.

Now suppose the sequence is infinite. Then every term is in $S$, so $v_2(a_n)\ge1$ for all $n$.  
If at any step we have $v_2=2$, then the next term is odd, which is allowed (odd numbers can be in $S$), but then from that odd number the sequence strictly decreases (as shown below), so it would eventually become prime or prime square, contradicting infiniteness. Hence $v_2$ can never be $2$ in an infinite sequence.  
Thus $v_2(a_n)$ is always $1$ or $\ge3$.  
If $v_2\ge3$, then $v_2$ strictly decreases each step until it becomes $1$ or $0$. If it becomes $0$, then the term is odd, and we are done (since from odd it will decrease to a prime). So to avoid odd, we must never hit $v_2=0$. Therefore, when $v_2$ becomes $1$, it must stay $1$ forever.  
So assume that from some point on, $v_2(a_n)=1$ for all $n$. Then each term is of the form $2\cdot m$ with $m$ odd. Let $p_n$ be the smallest odd prime factor of $m_n$. Since the sequence is not constant (otherwise it would be a fixed point, which is in $F$), we have $p_n\ge5$. Then $f(2m_n)=(p_n+3)m_n/p_n$. For $v_2$ to remain $1$, we need $v_2(p_n+3)=1$, i.e., $p_n\equiv3\pmod4$. So $p_n\equiv3\pmod4$. Moreover, the next odd part is $u_n=((p_n+3)/2)\cdot (m_n/p_n)$. The smallest odd prime factor of $u_n$ is either the smallest odd prime factor of $m_n/p_n$ (which is at least $p_n$) or the smallest odd prime factor of $(p_n+3)/2$. Since $p_n\equiv3\pmod4$, $(p_n+3)/2$ is odd and less than $p_n$ (for $p_n>3$). Hence the smallest odd prime factor of $u_n$ is strictly less than $p_n$. Therefore the sequence of smallest odd prime factors is a strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers. This cannot continue indefinitely. Hence after finitely many steps, we will have a term with $v_2=2$ or $v_2=0$. If $v_2=2$, then the next term is odd; if $v_2=0$, the term is odd. In either case, we obtain an odd term.  
Now, if a term is odd, say $N$ odd and composite (since it has at least three proper divisors), then the three largest proper divisors of $N$ are at most $N/3$, $N/5$, $N/7$, so $f(N)\le N/3+N/5+N/7 = 71N/105 < N$. Thus the sequence strictly decreases. Since it is a decreasing sequence of positive integers, it must eventually reach a number that is either prime or a prime square. But a prime or a prime square has fewer than three proper divisors, so the next term would be undefined, contradicting the assumption that the sequence is infinite. Therefore, the sequence cannot be infinite.  
Thus any $a_1$ not in $F\cup G$ leads to a finite sequence. Consequently, the only possible $a_1$ are those in $F\cup G$.

**Verification that $F\cup G$ indeed yields infinite sequences.**  
- If $a_1=6k$ with $k$ odd and $5\nmid k$, then $f(a_1)=a_1$, so the sequence is constant, thus infinite.
- If $a_1=72k$ with $k$ odd and $5\nmid k$, then $v_2(a_1)=3$ and the smallest odd prime factor is $3$. Hence $f(a_1)=13a_1/12=78k$. Since $k$ is odd and not divisible by $5$, $78k$ is in $F$ (because $78k/6=13k$ is odd and $5\nmid13k$). Therefore $f(78k)=78k$, so the sequence becomes constant at the fixed point $78k$. Hence the sequence is infinite.

Thus the set of all possible $a_1$ is exactly  
\[
\boxed{\{6k:\ k\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid k\}\ \cup\ \{72k:\ k\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid k\}}.
\]

Equivalently, $a_1$ is any positive integer such that either $a_1/6$ is odd and not divisible by $5$, or $a_1/72$ is odd and not divisible by $5$.
